<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Skyscraper</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Skyscraper_(disambiguation)" title="Skyscraper (disambiguation)">Skyscraper (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Burj_Khalifa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5740" data-file-width="3279" height="385" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/93/Burj_Khalifa.jpg/220px-Burj_Khalifa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/93/Burj_Khalifa.jpg/330px-Burj_Khalifa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/93/Burj_Khalifa.jpg/440px-Burj_Khalifa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Burj_Khalifa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Completed in 2009, the <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a> (<a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>), is currently the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">tallest skyscraper</a> in the world, with a height of 829.8 metres (2,722 ft). It shows a typical skyscraper feature: <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">setbacks</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A <b>skyscraper</b> is a tall, continuously habitable building having multiple <a href="/wiki/Storey" title="Storey">floors</a>. When the term was originally used in the 1880s it described a building of 10 to 20 floors but now describes one of at least 40–50 floors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> Mostly designed for office, commercial and residential uses, a skyscraper can also be called a <a href="/wiki/Tower_block" title="Tower block">high-rise</a>, but the term "skyscraper" is often used for buildings higher than 100 m (328 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-age-2">[2]</a></sup> For buildings above a height of 300 m (984 ft), the term "supertall" can be used, while skyscrapers reaching beyond 600 m (1,969 ft) are classified as "megatall".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CTBUH_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-CTBUH-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>One common feature of skyscrapers is having a steel framework that supports <a href="/wiki/Curtain_wall_(architecture)" title="Curtain wall (architecture)">curtain walls</a>. These curtain walls either bear on the framework below or are suspended from the framework above, rather than resting on <a href="/wiki/Load-bearing_wall" title="Load-bearing wall">load-bearing walls</a> of conventional construction. Some early skyscrapers have a <a href="/wiki/Steel_frame" title="Steel frame">steel frame</a> that enables the construction of load-bearing walls taller than of those made of <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a>.</p>
<p>Modern skyscrapers' walls are not load-bearing, and most skyscrapers are characterized by large surface areas of windows made possible by steel frames and curtain walls. However, skyscrapers can have curtain walls that mimic conventional walls with a small surface area of windows. Modern skyscrapers often have a <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">tubular structure</a>, and are designed to act like a hollow <a href="/wiki/Cylinder" title="Cylinder">cylinder</a> to resist wind, seismic, and other lateral loads. To appear more slender, allow less wind exposure, and transmit more daylight to the ground, many skyscrapers have a design with <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">setbacks</a>, which are sometimes also structurally required.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term "skyscraper" was first applied to buildings of steel framed construction of at least 10 <a href="/wiki/Storey" title="Storey">stories</a> in the late 19th century, a result of public amazement at the tall buildings being built in major cities like <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Detroit" title="Detroit">Detroit</a>, and <a href="/wiki/St._Louis" title="St. Louis">St. Louis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> The first steel-frame skyscraper was the <a href="/wiki/Home_Insurance_Building" title="Home Insurance Building">Home Insurance Building</a> (originally 10 stories with a height of 42 m or 138 ft) in Chicago, Illinois in 1885. Some point to Philadelphia's 10-story <a href="/wiki/William_L._Johnston" title="William L. Johnston">Jayne Building</a> (1849–50) as a proto-skyscraper, or to New York's seven-floor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equitable_Life_Building_(New_York_City)" title="Equitable Life Building (New York City)">Equitable Life Building (New York City)</a>, built in 1870, for its innovative use of a kind of skeletal frame, but such designation depends largely on what factors are chosen. Even the scholars making the argument find it to be purely academic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>The structural definition of the word <i>skyscraper</i> was refined later by architectural historians, based on engineering developments of the 1880s that had enabled construction of tall multi-story buildings. This definition was based on the steel skeleton—as opposed to constructions of load-bearing <a href="/wiki/Masonry" title="Masonry">masonry</a>, which passed their practical limit in 1891 with Chicago's <a href="/wiki/Monadnock_Building" title="Monadnock Building">Monadnock Building</a>.</p>
<table class="mbox-small plainlinks sistersitebox" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image"><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="355" data-file-width="300" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/34px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/51px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Wikiquote-logo.svg/68px-Wikiquote-logo.svg.png 2x" width="34"/></td>
<td class="mbox-text plainlist">Wikiquote has quotations related to: <i><b><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wikiquote.org/wiki/Special:Search/Skyscraper" title="q:Special:Search/Skyscraper">Skyscraper</a></b></i></td>
</tr>
</table>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>What is the chief characteristic of the tall office building? It is lofty. It must be tall. The force and power of altitude must be in it, the glory and pride of exaltation must be in it. It must be every inch a proud and soaring thing, rising in sheer exaltation that from bottom to top it is a unit without a single dissenting line.</p>
</blockquote>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>—<a href="/wiki/Louis_Sullivan" title="Louis Sullivan">Louis Sullivan</a>'s <i>The Tall Office Building Artistically Considered</i> (1896)</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emporis_Standards_Committee" title="Emporis Standards Committee">Emporis Standards Committee</a> defines a <a href="/wiki/Tower_block" title="Tower block">high-rise</a> building as "a multi-story structure between 35–100 meters tall, or a building of unknown height from 12–39 floors"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> and a skyscraper as "a multi-story building whose architectural height is at least 100 m or 330 ft."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Some <a href="/wiki/Structural_engineer" title="Structural engineer">structural engineers</a> define a highrise as any vertical construction for which wind is a more significant <a href="/wiki/Structural_load" title="Structural load">load factor</a> than <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquake</a> or weight. Note that this criterion fits not only high-rises but some other tall structures, such as <a href="/wiki/Tower" title="Tower">towers</a>.</p>
<p>The word <i>skyscraper</i> often carries a connotation of pride and achievement. The skyscraper, in name and social function, is a modern expression of the age-old symbol of the <a href="/wiki/Axis_mundi" title="Axis mundi">world center</a> or <i>axis mundi</i>: a pillar that connects earth to heaven and the four compass directions to one another.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<p>A loose convention of some in the United States and Europe draws the lower limit of a skyscraper at 150 m (490 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> But the term is also often used for buildings taller than 100 m (330 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-age-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>The tallest building in ancient times was the 146 m (479 ft) <a href="/wiki/Great_Pyramid_of_Giza" title="Great Pyramid of Giza">Great Pyramid of Giza</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">ancient Egypt</a>, built in the 26th century BC. It was not surpassed in height for thousands of years, the 160 m (520 ft) <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Cathedral" title="Lincoln Cathedral">Lincoln Cathedral</a> have exceeded it in 1311–1549, before central spire collapsed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> The latter in turn was not surpassed until the 555-foot (169 m) <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">Washington Monument</a> in 1884. However, being uninhabited, none of these structures actually comply with the modern definition of a skyscraper.</p>
<p>High-rise apartments flourished in <a href="/wiki/Classical_antiquity" title="Classical antiquity">classical antiquity</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">Ancient Roman</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insulae" title="Insulae">insulae</a> in <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">imperial</a> cities reached 10 and more stories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory_S._Aldrete_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_S._Aldrete-12">[12]</a></sup> Beginning with <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a> (r. 30 BC-14 AD), several <a href="/wiki/Roman_emperor" title="Roman emperor">emperors</a> attempted to establish limits of 20–25 m for multi-story buildings, but met with only limited success.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> Lower floors were typically occupied by shops or wealthy families, the upper rented to the lower classes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gregory_S._Aldrete_12-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gregory_S._Aldrete-12">[12]</a></sup> Surviving <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus_Papyri" title="Oxyrhynchus Papyri">Oxyrhynchus Papyri</a> indicate that seven-story buildings existed in <a href="/wiki/Roman_province" title="Roman province">provincial</a> towns such as in 3rd century AD <a href="/wiki/Hermopolis" title="Hermopolis">Hermopolis</a> in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Roman_Egypt" title="History of Roman Egypt">Roman Egypt</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>The skylines of many important <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval</a> cities had large numbers of high-rise urban towers, built by the wealthy for defense and status. The residential <a href="/wiki/Towers_of_Bologna" title="Towers of Bologna">Towers</a> of 12th century <a href="/wiki/Bologna" title="Bologna">Bologna</a> numbered between 80 and 100 at a time, the tallest of which is the 97.2 m (319 ft) high Asinelli Tower. A <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florentine</a> law of 1251 decreed that all urban buildings be immediately reduced to less than 26 m.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Werner_M.C3.BCller_345_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Werner_M.C3.BCller_345-16">[16]</a></sup> Even medium-sized towns of the era are known to have proliferations of towers, such as the 72 up to 51 m height in <a href="/wiki/San_Gimignano" title="San Gimignano">San Gimignano</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Werner_M.C3.BCller_345_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Werner_M.C3.BCller_345-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Arab_Egypt" title="History of Arab Egypt">medieval Egyptian</a> city of <a href="/wiki/Fustat" title="Fustat">Fustat</a> housed many high-rise residential buildings, which <a href="/wiki/Al-Muqaddasi" title="Al-Muqaddasi">Al-Muqaddasi</a> in the 10th century described as resembling <a href="/wiki/Minaret" title="Minaret">minarets</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nasir_Khusraw" title="Nasir Khusraw">Nasir Khusraw</a> in the early 11th century described some of them rising up to 14 stories, with <a href="/wiki/Roof_garden" title="Roof garden">roof gardens</a> on the top floor complete with ox-drawn <a href="/wiki/Water_wheel" title="Water wheel">water wheels</a> for irrigating them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a> in the 16th century had high-rise <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apartment_building" title="Apartment building">apartment buildings</a> where the two lower floors were for commercial and storage purposes and the multiple stories above them were <a href="/wiki/Renting" title="Renting">rented</a> out to <a href="/wiki/Leasehold_estate" title="Leasehold estate">tenants</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> An early example of a city consisting entirely of high-rise housing is the 16th-century city of <a href="/wiki/Shibam" title="Shibam">Shibam</a> in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>. Shibam was made up of over 500 tower houses,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-19">[19]</a></sup> each one rising 5 to 11 stories high,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> with each floor being an <a href="/wiki/Apartment" title="Apartment">apartment</a> occupied by a single family. The city was built in this way in order to protect it from <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> attacks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNESCO_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNESCO-19">[19]</a></sup> Shibam still has the tallest <a href="/wiki/Mudbrick" title="Mudbrick">mudbrick</a> buildings in the world, with many of them over 30 m (98 ft) high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<p>An early modern example of high-rise housing was in 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Edinburgh" title="Edinburgh">Edinburgh</a>, Scotland, where a defensive city wall defined the boundaries of the city. Due to the restricted land area available for development, the houses increased in height instead. Buildings of 11 stories were common, and there are records of buildings as high as 14 stories. Many of the stone-built structures can still be seen today in the old town of Edinburgh. The oldest iron framed building in the world, although only partially iron framed, is <a href="/wiki/Ditherington_Flax_Mill" title="Ditherington Flax Mill">The Flaxmill</a> (also locally known as the "Maltings"), in <a href="/wiki/Shrewsbury" title="Shrewsbury">Shrewsbury</a>, England. Built in 1797, it is seen as the "grandfather of skyscrapers", since its fireproof combination of cast iron columns and cast iron beams developed into the modern steel frame that made modern skyscrapers possible. In 2013 funding was confirmed to convert the derelict building into offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Oriel_chambers.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2401" data-file-width="1836" height="235" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Oriel_chambers.jpg/180px-Oriel_chambers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Oriel_chambers.jpg/270px-Oriel_chambers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Oriel_chambers.jpg/360px-Oriel_chambers.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Oriel_chambers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3576" data-file-width="3192" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg/180px-Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg/270px-Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg/360px-Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wainwright_building_st_louis_USA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Wainwright_Building" title="Wainwright Building">Wainwright Building</a>, a 10-story red brick office building in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Louis,_Missouri" title="St. Louis, Missouri">St. Louis, Missouri</a>, built in 1891</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_skyscrapers">Early skyscrapers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early skyscrapers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_skyscrapers" title="Early skyscrapers">Early skyscrapers</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_0" style="width:220px;" videopayload='&lt;div class="mediaContainer" style="width:600px"&gt;&lt;video id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP352_-_Cameraman_films_sky_scraper_-_EYE_FLM7657_-_OB_104841.ogv/600px--Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP352_-_Cameraman_films_sky_scraper_-_EYE_FLM7657_-_OB_104841.ogv.jpg" controls="" preload="none" autoplay="" style="width:600px;height:480px" class="kskin" data-durationhint="51" data-startoffset="0" data-mwtitle="Bits_&amp;amp;_Pieces_-_BP352_-_Cameraman_films_sky_scraper_-_EYE_FLM7657_-_OB_104841.ogv" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"&gt;&lt;source src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/e/ec/Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP352_-_Cameraman_films_sky_scraper_-_EYE_FLM7657_-_OB_104841.ogv/Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP352_-_Cameraman_films_sky_scraper_-_EYE_FLM7657_-_OB_104841.ogv.480p.webm" type="video/webm; <div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bits_%26_Pieces_-_BP352_-_Cameraman_films_sky_scraper_-_EYE_FLM7657_-_OB_104841.ogv" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Cameraman films skyscraper.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1857 <a href="/wiki/Elisha_Otis" title="Elisha Otis">Elisha Otis</a> introduced the safety elevator, allowing convenient and safe passenger movement to upper floors. Another crucial development was the use of a steel frame instead of stone or brick, otherwise the walls on the lower floors on a tall building would be too thick to be practical. An early development in this area was <a href="/wiki/Oriel_Chambers" title="Oriel Chambers">Oriel Chambers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Liverpool" title="Liverpool">Liverpool</a>, England. It was only five floors high.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Further developments led to the world's first skyscraper, the ten-story <a href="/wiki/Home_Insurance_Building" title="Home Insurance Building">Home Insurance Building</a> in Chicago, built in 1884–1885.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Verbivore.27s_Feast.2C_p289_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Verbivore.27s_Feast.2C_p289-26">[26]</a></sup> While its height is not considered very impressive today, it was at that time. The building of tall buildings in the 1880s gave the skyscraper its first architectural movement the <a href="/wiki/Chicago_school_(architecture)" title="Chicago school (architecture)">Chicago School</a>, which developed what has been called the Commercial Style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>The architect, Major <a href="/wiki/William_Le_Baron_Jenney" title="William Le Baron Jenney">William Le Baron Jenney</a>, created a load-bearing structural frame. In this building, a steel frame supported the entire weight of the walls, instead of load-bearing walls carrying the weight of the building. This development led to the "Chicago skeleton" form of construction. In addition to the steel frame, the Home Insurance Building also utilized fireproofing, elevators, and electrical wiring, key elements in most skyscrapers today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Burnham_and_Root" title="Burnham and Root">Burnham and Root</a>'s 45 m (148 ft) <a href="/wiki/Rand_McNally_Building" title="Rand McNally Building">Rand McNally Building</a> in Chicago, 1889, was the first all-steel framed skyscraper,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Louis_Sullivan" title="Louis Sullivan">Louis Sullivan</a>'s 41 m (135 ft) <a href="/wiki/Wainwright_Building" title="Wainwright Building">Wainwright Building</a> in St. Louis, Missouri, 1891, was the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Steel-framed_building" title="Steel-framed building">steel-framed building</a> with soaring vertical bands to emphasize the height of the building and is therefore considered to be the first early skyscraper.</p>
<p>Most early skyscrapers emerged in the land-strapped areas of Chicago and New York City toward the end of the 19th century. A land boom in Melbourne, Australia between 1888 and 1891 spurred the creation of a significant number of early skyscrapers, though none of these were steel reinforced and few remain today. Height limits and fire restrictions were later introduced. London builders soon found building heights limited due to a complaint from <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>, rules that continued to exist with few exceptions until the 1950s.</p>
<p>Concerns about <a href="/wiki/Aesthetics" title="Aesthetics">aesthetics</a> and fire safety had likewise hampered the development of skyscrapers across continental Europe for the first half of the twentieth century. Some notable exceptions are the 43 m (141 ft) tall 1898 <a href="/wiki/Witte_Huis" title="Witte Huis">Witte Huis</a> <i>(White House)</i> in <a href="/wiki/Rotterdam" title="Rotterdam">Rotterdam</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Liver_Building" title="Royal Liver Building">Royal Liver Building</a> in Liverpool, completed in 1911 and 90 m (300 ft) high;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> the 57 m (187 ft) tall 1924 <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Marx_House" title="Wilhelm Marx House">Marx House</a> in <a href="/wiki/D%C3%BCsseldorf" title="Düsseldorf">Düsseldorf</a>, Germany; the 61 m (200 ft) <a href="/wiki/Kungstornen" title="Kungstornen">Kungstornen</a> <i>(Kings' Towers)</i> in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm</a>, Sweden, which were built 1924–25,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> the 89 m (292 ft) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edificio_Telef%C3%B3nica" title="Edificio Telefónica">Edificio Telefónica</a> in <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a>, Spain, built in 1929; the 87.5 m (287 ft) <a href="/wiki/Boerentoren" title="Boerentoren">Boerentoren</a> in Antwerp, Belgium, built in 1932; the 66 m (217 ft) <a href="/wiki/Prudential,_Warsaw" title="Prudential, Warsaw">Prudential Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/Warsaw" title="Warsaw">Warsaw</a>, Poland, built in 1934; and the 108 m (354 ft) <a href="/wiki/Terrazza_Martini_Tower" title="Terrazza Martini Tower">Torre Piacentini</a> in <a href="/wiki/Genoa" title="Genoa">Genoa</a>, Italy, built in 1940.</p>
<p>After an early competition between Chicago and New York City for the world's tallest building, New York took the lead by 1895 with the completion of the 103 m (338 ft) long <a href="/wiki/American_Surety_Building" title="American Surety Building">American Surety Building</a>, leaving New York with the title of the world's tallest building for many years.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_skyscrapers">Modern skyscrapers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Modern skyscrapers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg/220px-Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg/330px-Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg/440px-Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flatiron_Building_3618433845_5745ebc1b9.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flatiron_building" title="Flatiron building">Flatiron building</a> completed in 1902 in New York City</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1014" data-file-width="756" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg/180px-Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg/270px-Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg/360px-Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Empire_State_Building_from_the_Top_of_the_Rock.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a> in New York City. Completed in 1931, it was the tallest building in the world for nearly 40 years.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Modern skyscrapers are built with <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> or <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a> frameworks and <a href="/wiki/Curtain_wall_(architecture)" title="Curtain wall (architecture)">curtain walls</a> of <a href="/wiki/Glass" title="Glass">glass</a> or polished <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stone" title="Stone">stone</a>. They use mechanical equipment such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_pump" title="Water pump">water pumps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">elevators</a>.</p>
<p>From the 1930s onwards, skyscrapers began to appear around the world—such as in Latin America (such as <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Paulo" title="São Paulo">São Paulo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buenos_Aires" title="Buenos Aires">Buenos Aires</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Santiago,_Chile" title="Santiago, Chile">Santiago</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lima" title="Lima">Lima</a>, <a href="/wiki/Caracas" title="Caracas">Caracas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bogot%C3%A1" title="Bogotá">Bogotá</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama_City" title="Panama City">Panama City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mexico_City" title="Mexico City">Mexico City</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monterrey" title="Monterrey">Monterrey</a>) and in Asia (<a href="/wiki/Tokyo" title="Tokyo">Tokyo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shanghai" title="Shanghai">Shanghai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hong_Kong" title="Hong Kong">Hong Kong</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">Manila</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jakarta" title="Jakarta">Jakarta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mumbai" title="Mumbai">Mumbai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Taipei" title="Taipei">Taipei</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">Bangkok</a>).</p>
<p>Immediately after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> planned eight massive skyscrapers, seven of which were built by 1953, dubbed the "<a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(Moscow)" title="Seven Sisters (Moscow)">Seven Sisters of Moscow</a>". The <a href="/wiki/Main_building_of_Moscow_State_University" title="Main building of Moscow State University">Building of Moscow State University</a> was the tallest building in Europe in 1953–1990. Other skyscrapers in the style of <a href="/wiki/Stalinist_architecture" title="Stalinist architecture">Socialist Classicism</a> were erected in East Germany (<a href="/wiki/Frankfurter_Tor" title="Frankfurter Tor">Frankfurter Tor</a>), Poland (<a href="/wiki/Palace_of_Culture_and_Science" title="Palace of Culture and Science">PKiN</a>), Ukraine (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hotel_Ukrayina" title="Hotel Ukrayina">Hotel Ukrayina</a>), Latvia (<a href="/wiki/Latvian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Latvian Academy of Sciences">Academy of Sciences</a>) and other countries. The western countries of Europe also began to permit taller skyscrapers than before WW2, such as <a href="/wiki/Madrid" title="Madrid">Madrid</a> during the 1950s (<a href="/wiki/Gran_V%C3%ADa_(Madrid)" title="Gran Vía (Madrid)">Gran Vía</a>). Finally, skyscrapers also began to be constructed in cities of Africa, the Middle East and Oceania (mainly Australia) from the late 1950s on.</p>
<p>Skyscraper projects after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> typically rejected the classical designs of the <a href="/wiki/Early_skyscrapers" title="Early skyscrapers">early skyscrapers</a>, instead embracing the uniform <a href="/wiki/International_Style_(architecture)" title="International Style (architecture)">international style</a>; many older skyscrapers were redesigned to suit contemporary tastes or even demolished—such as New York's <a href="/wiki/Singer_Building" title="Singer Building">Singer Building</a>, once the world's tallest skyscraper.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">German</a> architect <a href="/wiki/Ludwig_Mies_van_der_Rohe" title="Ludwig Mies van der Rohe">Ludwig Mies van der Rohe</a> became one of the world's most renowned architects in the second half of the 20th century. He conceived of the glass façade skyscraper<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and, along with Norwegian <a href="/wiki/Fred_Severud" title="Fred Severud">Fred Severud</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> he designed the <a href="/wiki/Seagram_Building" title="Seagram Building">Seagram Building</a> in 1958, a skyscraper that is often regarded as the pinnacle of the modernist high-rise architecture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-obit_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-obit-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a> skyscrapers construction suffered a hiatus for over thirty years due to economic problems. A revival occurred with structural innovations that transformed the industry,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-35">[35]</a></sup> making it possible for people to live and work in "cities in the sky".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the early 1960s structural engineer <a href="/wiki/Fazlur_Rahman_Khan" title="Fazlur Rahman Khan">Fazlur Rahman Khan</a>, considered the "father of <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">tubular designs</a>" for high-rises,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> realized that the dominating rigid <a href="/wiki/Steel_frame" title="Steel frame">steel frame</a> structure was not the only system apt for tall buildings, marking a new era of skyscraper construction in terms of multiple <a href="/wiki/Structural_system" title="Structural system">structural systems</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> His central innovation in <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper_design_and_construction" title="Skyscraper design and construction">skyscraper design and construction</a> was the concept of the <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">"tube" structural system</a>, including the "framed tube", "trussed tube", and "bundled tube".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ali_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-39">[39]</a></sup> These systems allow greater economic efficiency,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-40">[40]</a></sup> and also allow skyscrapers to take on various shapes, no longer needing to be rectangular and box-shaped.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayley_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bayley-41">[41]</a></sup> The first building to employ the tube structure was the <a href="/wiki/Plaza_on_DeWitt" title="Plaza on DeWitt">Chestnut De-Witt</a> apartment building.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-35">[35]</a></sup> Over the next fifteen years, many towers were built by Fazlur Rahman Khan and the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chicago_School_(architecture)" title="Chicago School (architecture)">Second Chicago School</a>",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Billington_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Billington-42">[42]</a></sup> including the massive 442 m (1,450 ft) <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Other pioneers of this field include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Srinivasa_%27Hal%27_Iyengar" title="Srinivasa 'Hal' Iyengar">Hal Iyengar</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_LeMessurier" title="William LeMessurier">William LeMessurier</a>.</p>
<p>Modern building practices regarding supertall structures have led to the study of "vanity height".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Vanity height, according to the CTBUH, is the distance between the highest floor and its architectural top (excluding antennae, flagpole or other functional extensions). Vanity height first appeared in New York City skyscrapers as early as the 1920s and 1930s but supertall buildings have relied on such uninhabitable extensions for on average 30% of their height, raising potential definitional and sustainability issues.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> The current era of skyscrapers focuses on <a href="/wiki/Sustainability" title="Sustainability">sustainability</a>, its built and natural environments, including the performance of structures, types of materials, construction practices, absolute minimal use of materials and natural resources, energy within the structure, and a holistically integrated building systems approach. <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">LEED</a> is a current <a href="/wiki/Green_building" title="Green building">green building</a> standard.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Architecturally, with the movements of <a href="/wiki/Postmodern_architecture" title="Postmodern architecture">Postmodernism</a>, <a href="/wiki/New_Urbanism" title="New Urbanism">New Urbanism</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Classical_Architecture" title="New Classical Architecture">New Classical Architecture</a>, that established since the 1980s, a more classical approach came back to global skyscraper design, that remains popular today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> Examples are the <a href="/wiki/Wells_Fargo_Center_(Minneapolis)" title="Wells Fargo Center (Minneapolis)">Wells Fargo Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/NBC_Tower" title="NBC Tower">NBC Tower</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parkview_Square" title="Parkview Square">Parkview Square</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/30_Park_Place" title="30 Park Place">30 Park Place</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Messeturm" title="Messeturm">Messeturm</a>, the iconic <a href="/wiki/Petronas_Towers" title="Petronas Towers">Petronas Towers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jin_Mao_Tower" title="Jin Mao Tower">Jin Mao Tower</a>.</p>
<p>Other contemporary styles and movements in skyscraper design include <a href="/wiki/Organic_architecture" title="Organic architecture">organic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neo-Futurism" title="Neo-Futurism">neo-futurist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Structuralism_(architecture)" title="Structuralism (architecture)">structuralist</a>, <a href="/wiki/High-tech_architecture" title="High-tech architecture">high-tech</a>, <a href="/wiki/Deconstructivism" title="Deconstructivism">deconstructivist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Blobitecture" title="Blobitecture">blob</a>, <a href="/wiki/Digital_architecture" title="Digital architecture">digital</a>, <a href="/wiki/Streamline_Moderne" title="Streamline Moderne">streamline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novelty_architecture" title="Novelty architecture">novelty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critical_regionalism" title="Critical regionalism">critical regionalist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vernacular_architecture" title="Vernacular architecture">vernacular</a>, <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Neo Art Deco</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Historism" title="Neo-Historism">neo-historist</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Revivalism_(architecture)" title="Revivalism (architecture)">revivalist</a>.</p>
<p>3 September is the global commemorative day for skyscrapers, called "Skyscraper Day".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>New York City developers competed among themselves, with successively taller buildings claiming the title of "world's tallest" in the 1920s and early 1930s, culminating with the completion of the 318.9 m (1,046 ft) <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> in 1930 and the 443.2 m (1,454 ft) <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a> in 1931, the world's tallest building for forty years. The first completed 417 m (1,368 ft) tall <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a> tower became the world's tallest building in 1972. However, it was overtaken by the Sears Tower (now <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a>) in Chicago within two years. The 442 m (1,450 ft) tall Sears Tower stood as the world's tallest building for 24 years, from 1974 until 1998, until it was edged out by 452 m (1,483 ft) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petronas_Twin_Towers" title="Petronas Twin Towers">Petronas Twin Towers</a> in Kuala Lumpur, which held the title for six years.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Design_and_construction">Design and construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Design and construction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper_design_and_construction" title="Skyscraper design and construction">Skyscraper design and construction</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg/220px-Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg/330px-Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg/440px-Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skyscraper_Los_Angeles_Downtown_2013.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Modern Skyscrapers in <a href="/wiki/Downtown_Los_Angeles" title="Downtown Los Angeles">Downtown Los Angeles</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The design and construction of skyscrapers involves creating safe, habitable spaces in very tall buildings. The buildings must support their weight, resist wind and earthquakes, and protect occupants from fire. Yet they must also be conveniently accessible, even on the upper floors, and provide utilities and a comfortable climate for the occupants. The problems posed in skyscraper design are considered among the most complex encountered given the balances required between <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Civil_Engineering" title="Civil Engineering">engineering</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Construction" title="Construction">construction</a> management.</p>
<p>One common feature of skyscrapers is a steel framework from which curtain walls are suspended, rather than load-bearing walls of conventional construction. Most skyscrapers have a steel frame that enables them to be built taller than typical load-bearing walls of reinforced concrete. Skyscrapers usually have a particularly small surface area of what are conventionally thought of as walls. Because the walls are not load-bearing most skyscrapers are characterized by surface areas of windows made possible by the concept of steel frame and curtain wall. However, skyscrapers can also have curtain walls that mimick conventional walls and have a small surface area of windows.</p>
<p>The concept of a skyscraper is a product of the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_society" title="Industrial society">industrialized age</a>, made possible by cheap <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuel</a> derived energy and industrially refined raw materials such as <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Concrete" title="Concrete">concrete</a>. The construction of skyscrapers was enabled by <a href="/wiki/Steel_frame" title="Steel frame">steel frame</a> construction that surpassed <a href="/wiki/Brick_and_mortar" title="Brick and mortar">brick and mortar</a> construction starting at the end of the 19th century and finally surpassing it in the 20th century together with <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced concrete</a> construction as the price of steel decreased and labour costs increased.</p>
<p>The steel frames become inefficient and uneconomic for supertall buildings as usable floor space is reduced for progressively larger supporting columns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehigh.edu_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-lehigh.edu-52">[52]</a></sup> Since about 1960, tubular designs have been used for high rises. This reduces the usage of material (more efficient in economic terms – <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a> uses a third less steel than the Empire State Building) yet allows greater height. It allows fewer interior columns, and so creates more usable floor space. It further enables buildings to take on various shapes.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">Elevators</a> are characteristic to skyscrapers. In 1852 Elisha Otis introduced the safety elevator, allowing convenient and safe passenger movement to upper floors. Another crucial development was the use of a steel frame instead of stone or brick, otherwise the walls on the lower floors on a tall building would be too thick to be practical. Today major manufacturers of elevators include <a href="/wiki/Otis_Elevator_Company" title="Otis Elevator Company">Otis</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ThyssenKrupp_AG" title="ThyssenKrupp AG">ThyssenKrupp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Schindler_Group" title="Schindler Group">Schindler</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/KONE" title="KONE">KONE</a>.</p>
<p>Advances in construction techniques have allowed skyscrapers to narrow in width, while increasing in height. Some of these new techniques include mass dampers to reduce vibrations and swaying, and gaps to allow air to pass through, reducing wind shear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Basic_design_considerations">Basic design considerations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Basic design considerations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Good structural design is important in most building design, but particularly for skyscrapers since even a small chance of catastrophic failure is unacceptable given the high price. This presents a paradox to civil engineers: the only way to assure a lack of failure is to test for all modes of failure, in both the laboratory and the real world. But the only way to know of all modes of failure is to learn from previous failures. Thus, no engineer can be absolutely sure that a given structure will resist all loadings that could cause failure, but can only have large enough margins of safety such that a failure is acceptably unlikely. When buildings do fail, engineers question whether the failure was due to some lack of foresight or due to some unknowable factor.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Loading_and_vibration">Loading and vibration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Loading and vibration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The load a skyscraper experiences is largely from the force of the building material itself. In most building designs, the weight of the structure is much larger than the weight of the material that it will support beyond its own weight. In technical terms, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dead_load" title="Dead load">dead load</a>, the load of the structure, is larger than the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Live_load" title="Live load">live load</a>, the weight of things in the structure (people, furniture, vehicles, etc.). As such, the amount of structural material required within the lower levels of a skyscraper will be much larger than the material required within higher levels. This is not always visually apparent. The <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Setback_(architecture)" title="Setback (architecture)">setbacks</a> are actually a result of the building code at the time (<a href="/wiki/1916_Zoning_Resolution" title="1916 Zoning Resolution">1916 Zoning Resolution</a>), and were not structurally required. On the other hand, <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Center" title="John Hancock Center">John Hancock Center</a>'s shape is uniquely the result of how it supports loads. Vertical supports can come in several types, among which the most common for skyscrapers can be categorized as steel frames, concrete cores, tube within tube design, and shear walls.</p>
<p>The wind loading on a skyscraper is also considerable. In fact, the lateral wind load imposed on super-tall structures is generally the governing factor in the structural design. Wind pressure increases with height, so for very tall buildings, the loads associated with wind are larger than dead or live loads.</p>
<p>Other vertical and horizontal loading factors come from varied, unpredictable sources, such as earthquakes.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Steel_frame">Steel frame</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Steel frame">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1895, <a href="/wiki/Steel" title="Steel">steel</a> had replaced <a href="/wiki/Cast_iron" title="Cast iron">cast iron</a> as skyscrapers' structural material. Its malleability allowed it to be formed into a variety of shapes, and it could be riveted, ensuring strong connections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> The simplicity of a steel frame eliminated the inefficient part of a shear wall, the central portion, and consolidated support members in a much stronger fashion by allowing both horizontal and vertical supports throughout. Among steel's drawbacks is that as more material must be supported as height increases, the distance between supporting members must decrease, which in turn increases the amount of material that must be supported. This becomes inefficient and uneconomic for buildings above 40 stories tall as usable floor spaces are reduced for supporting column and due to more usage of steel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehigh.edu_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-lehigh.edu-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tube_structural_systems">Tube structural systems</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Tube structural systems">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">Tube (structure)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3196" data-file-width="1597" height="360" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg/180px-KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg/270px-KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg/360px-KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KM_5939_sears_tower_august_2007_B.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a> in Chicago showing the bundled tube frame design</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A new structural system of framed tubes was developed in 1963. Fazlur Khan and J. Rankine defined the framed tube structure as "a three dimensional space structure composed of three, four, or possibly more frames, braced frames, or shear walls, joined at or near their edges to form a vertical tube-like structural system capable of resisting lateral forces in any direction by cantilevering from the foundation."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Closely spaced interconnected exterior columns form the tube. Horizontal loads (primarily wind) are supported by the structure as a whole. Framed tubes allow fewer interior columns, and so create more usable floor space, and about half the exterior surface is available for windows. Where larger openings like garage doors are required, the tube frame must be interrupted, with transfer girders used to maintain structural integrity. Tube structures cut down costs, at the same time allowing buildings to reach greater heights. Concrete tube-frame construction<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ali_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-39">[39]</a></sup> was first used in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/DeWitt-Chestnut_Apartment_Building" title="DeWitt-Chestnut Apartment Building">DeWitt-Chestnut Apartment Building</a>, completed in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> in 1963,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup> and soon after in the <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Center" title="John Hancock Center">John Hancock Center</a> and <a href="/wiki/Construction_of_the_World_Trade_Center" title="Construction of the World Trade Center">World Trade Center</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">tubular systems</a> are fundamental to tall building design. Most buildings over 40-stories constructed since the 1960s now use a tube design derived from Khan's structural engineering principles,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lehigh.edu_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-lehigh.edu-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-constructionweekonline.com_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-constructionweekonline.com-57">[57]</a></sup> examples including the <a href="/wiki/Construction_of_the_World_Trade_Center" title="Construction of the World Trade Center">construction of the World Trade Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aon_Center_(Chicago)" title="Aon Center (Chicago)">Aon Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Petronas_Towers" title="Petronas Towers">Petronas Towers</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jin_Mao_Building" title="Jin Mao Building">Jin Mao Building</a>, and most other supertall skyscrapers since the 1960s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ali_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ali-39">[39]</a></sup> The strong influence of tube structure design is also evident in the construction of the current tallest skyscraper, the <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayley_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bayley-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Trussed_tube_and_X-bracing">Trussed tube and X-bracing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Trussed tube and X-bracing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skyscraper_structure.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="431" data-file-width="675" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Skyscraper_structure.png/220px-Skyscraper_structure.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Skyscraper_structure.png/330px-Skyscraper_structure.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Skyscraper_structure.png/440px-Skyscraper_structure.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skyscraper_structure.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Changes of structure with height. The <a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">tubular systems</a> are fundamental for super tall buildings.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Khan pioneered several other variations of the tube structure design.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> One of these was the concept of <a href="/wiki/X-bracing" title="X-bracing">X-bracing</a>, or the "<a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)#Trussed_tube" title="Tube (structure)">trussed tube</a>", first employed for the <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Center" title="John Hancock Center">John Hancock Center</a>. This concept reduced the lateral load on the building by transferring the load into the exterior columns. This allows for a reduced need for interior columns thus creating more floor space. This concept can be seen in the John Hancock Center, designed in 1965 and completed in 1969. One of the most famous buildings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Structural_Expressionism" title="Structural Expressionism">structural expressionist</a> style, the skyscraper's distinctive X-bracing exterior is actually a hint that the structure's skin is indeed part of its 'tubular system'. This idea is one of the architectural techniques the building used to climb to record heights (the tubular system is essentially the spine that helps the building stand upright during wind and <a href="/wiki/Seismic_loading" title="Seismic loading">earthquake loads</a>). This X-bracing allows for both higher performance from tall structures and the ability to open up the inside floorplan (and usable floor space) if the architect desires.</p>
<p>The John Hancock Center was far more efficient than earlier <a href="/wiki/Steel_frame" title="Steel frame">steel-frame</a> structures. Where the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a> (1931), required about 206 kilograms of steel per square metre and <a href="/wiki/Chase_Manhattan_Bank_Building" title="Chase Manhattan Bank Building">Chase Manhattan Bank Building</a> (1961) required 275, the John Hancock Center required only 145.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_40-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-40">[40]</a></sup> The trussed tube concept was applied to many later skyscrapers, including the <a href="/wiki/Onterie_Center" title="Onterie Center">Onterie Center</a>, <a href="/wiki/Citigroup_Center" title="Citigroup Center">Citigroup Center</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bank_of_China_Tower,_Hong_Kong" title="Bank of China Tower, Hong Kong">Bank of China Tower</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Introduction_to_Tall_Building_architectures_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Introduction_to_Tall_Building_architectures-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bundled_tube">Bundled tube</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Bundled tube">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>An important variation on the tube frame is the "<a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)#Bundled_tube" title="Tube (structure)">bundled tube</a>", which uses several interconnected tube frames. The <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a> in Chicago used this design, employing nine tubes of varying height to achieve its distinct appearance. The bundled tube structure meant that "buildings no longer need be boxlike in appearance: they could become sculpture."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bayley_41-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bayley-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_elevator_conundrum">The elevator conundrum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: The elevator conundrum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The invention of the <a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">elevator</a> was a precondition for the invention of skyscrapers, given that most people would not (or could not) climb more than a few flights of stairs at a time. The elevators in a skyscraper are not simply a necessary utility, like running water and electricity, but are in fact closely related to the design of the whole structure: a taller building requires more elevators to service the additional floors, but the elevator shafts consume valuable floor space. If the service core, which contains the elevator shafts, becomes too big, it can reduce the profitability of the building. Architects must therefore balance the value gained by adding height against the value lost to the expanding service core.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HSW3_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-HSW3-59">[59]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many tall buildings use elevators in a non-standard configuration to reduce their footprint. Buildings such as the former <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center Towers</a> and Chicago's <a href="/wiki/John_Hancock_Center" title="John Hancock Center">John Hancock Center</a> use <a href="/wiki/Sky_lobby" title="Sky lobby">sky lobbies</a>, where express elevators take passengers to upper floors which serve as the base for local elevators. This allows architects and engineers to place elevator shafts on top of each other, saving space. Sky lobbies and express elevators take up a significant amount of space, however, and add to the amount of time spent commuting between floors.</p>
<p>Other buildings, such as the <a href="/wiki/Petronas_Towers" title="Petronas Towers">Petronas Towers</a>, use <a href="/wiki/Double-deck_elevator" title="Double-deck elevator">double-deck elevators</a>, allowing more people to fit in a single elevator, and reaching two floors at every stop. It is possible to use even more than two levels on an elevator, although this has never been done. The main problem with double-deck elevators is that they cause everyone in the elevator to stop when only people on one level need to get off at a given floor.</p>
<p>Buildings with sky lobbies include the <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(2001%E2%80%93present)" title="World Trade Center (2001–present)">World Trade Center</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petronas_Twin_Towers" title="Petronas Twin Towers">Petronas Twin Towers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a>. The 44th-floor sky lobby of the John Hancock Center also featured the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/High-rise" title="High-rise">high-rise</a> indoor <a href="/wiki/Swimming_pool" title="Swimming pool">swimming pool</a>, which remains the highest in America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emporis_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emporis-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_rationale">Economic rationale</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Economic rationale">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Skyscrapers are usually situated in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/City_center" title="City center">city centers</a> where the price of land is high. Constructing a skyscraper becomes justified if the price of land is so high that it makes <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economic</a> sense to build upwards as to minimize the cost of the land per the total floor area of a building. Thus the construction of skyscrapers is dictated by economics and results in skyscrapers in a certain part of a large city unless a <a href="/wiki/Building_code" title="Building code">building code</a> restricts the height of buildings.</p>
<p>Skyscrapers are rarely seen in small cities and they are characteristic of large cities, because of the critical importance of high land prices for the construction of skyscrapers. Usually only office, commercial and hotel users can afford the rents in the city center and thus most tenants of skyscrapers are of these classes. Some skyscrapers have been built in areas where the <a href="/wiki/Bedrock" title="Bedrock">bedrock</a> is near surface, because this makes constructing the <a href="/wiki/Foundation_(engineering)" title="Foundation (engineering)">foundation</a> cheaper, for example this is the case in <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lower_Manhattan" title="Lower Manhattan">Lower Manhattan</a>, in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, but not in-between these two parts of the city.</p>
<p>Today, skyscrapers are an increasingly common sight where land is expensive, as in the centers of big cities, because they provide such a high ratio of rentable floor space per unit area of land.</p>
<p><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">

<p>One problem with skyscrapers is <a href="/wiki/Parking_lot" title="Parking lot">car parking</a>. In the largest cities most people commute via public transport, but for smaller cities a lot of parking spaces are needed. <a href="/wiki/Multi-storey_car_park" title="Multi-storey car park">Multi-storey car parks</a> are impractical to build very tall, so a lot of land area is needed.</p>
<p>There may be a correlation between skyscraper construction and great <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Income_inequality" title="Income inequality">income inequality</a> but this has not been conclusively proved.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_impact">Environmental impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Environmental impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text">
