<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3869" data-file-width="2003" height="348" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg/180px-30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg/270px-30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg/360px-30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:30_St_Mary_Axe_-_The_Gherkin_from_Leadenhall_St_-_Nov_2006.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/30_St_Mary_Axe" title="30 St Mary Axe">30 St Mary Axe</a> in London is an example of a modern environmentally friendly skyscraper.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The amount of steel, concrete and glass needed to construct a single skyscraper is large, and these materials represent a great deal of <a href="/wiki/Embodied_energy" title="Embodied energy">embodied energy</a>. Skyscrapers are thus energy intensive buildings, but skyscrapers have a long lifespan, for example the <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> completed in 1931 and is still in active use.</p>
<p>Skyscrapers have considerable mass, which means that they must be built on a sturdier foundation than would be required for shorter, lighter buildings. Building materials must also be lifted to the top of a skyscraper during construction, requiring more energy than would be necessary at lower heights. Furthermore, a skyscraper consumes a lot of electricity because <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Potable" title="Potable">potable</a> and non-potable water have to be pumped to the highest occupied floors, skyscrapers are usually designed to be <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">mechanically ventilated</a>, elevators are generally used instead of stairs, and natural lighting cannot be utilized in rooms far from the windows and the windowless spaces such as elevators, bathrooms and stairwells.</p>
<p>Skyscrapers can be artificially lighted and the energy requirements can be covered by <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> or other electricity generation of low <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">greenhouse gas</a> emissions. Heating and cooling of skyscrapers can be efficient, because of centralized <a href="/wiki/HVAC" title="HVAC">HVAC</a> systems, heat radiation blocking <a href="/wiki/Window" title="Window">windows</a> and small surface area of the building. There is <a href="/wiki/Leadership_in_Energy_and_Environmental_Design" title="Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design">Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design</a> (LEED) certification for skyscrapers. For example, the Empire State Building received a gold Leadership in Energy and Environmental Design rating in September 2011 and the Empire State Building is the tallest LEED certified building in the United States,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> proving that skyscrapers can be environmentally friendly. Also the <a href="/wiki/30_St_Mary_Axe" title="30 St Mary Axe">30 St Mary Axe</a> in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> is an environmentally friendly skyscraper.</p>
<p>In the lower levels of a skyscraper a larger percentage of the building cross section must be devoted to the building structure and services than is required for lower buildings:</p>
<ul>
<li>More structure – because it must be stronger to support more floors above</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Skyscraper_design_and_construction#The_elevator_conundrum" title="Skyscraper design and construction">The elevator conundrum</a> creates the need for more lift shafts—everyone comes in at the bottom and they all have to pass through the lower part of the building to get to the upper levels.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Building_services" title="Building services">Building services</a> – power and water enter the building from below and have to pass through the lower levels to get to the upper levels.</li>
</ul>
<p>In low-rise structures, the support rooms (<a href="/wiki/Chiller" title="Chiller">chillers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transformer" title="Transformer">transformers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Boiler" title="Boiler">boilers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pump" title="Pump">pumps</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_handling_unit" title="Air handling unit">air handling units</a>) can be put in basements or roof space—areas which have low rental value. There is, however, a limit to how far this plant can be located from the area it serves. The farther away it is the larger the risers for ducts and pipes from this plant to the floors they serve and the more floor area these risers take. In practice this means that in highrise buildings this plant is located on 'plant levels' at intervals up the building.</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Bird-skyscraper_collisions" title="Bird-skyscraper collisions">Bird-skyscraper collisions</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_the_tallest_skyscrapers">History of the tallest skyscrapers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: History of the tallest skyscrapers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_the_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="History of the tallest buildings in the world">History of the tallest buildings in the world</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_world" title="List of tallest buildings in the world">List of tallest buildings in the world</a></div>
<p>At the beginning of the 20th century, <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> was a center for the <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">Beaux-Arts architectural</a> movement, attracting the talents of such great architects as <a href="/wiki/Stanford_White" title="Stanford White">Stanford White</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carrere_and_Hastings" title="Carrere and Hastings">Carrere and Hastings</a>. As better construction and engineering technology became available as the century progressed, New York City and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a> became the focal point of the competition for the tallest building in the world. Each city's striking skyline has been composed of numerous and varied skyscrapers, many of which are icons of 20th-century architecture:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Flatiron_Building" title="Flatiron Building">Flatiron Building</a>, designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daniel_Hudson_Burnham" title="Daniel Hudson Burnham">Daniel Hudson Burnham</a> and standing 285 ft (87 m) high, was one of the tallest buildings in New York City upon its completion in 1902, made possible by its steel skeleton. It was one of the first buildings designed with a steel framework, and to achieve this height with other construction methods of that time would have been very difficult. (The <a href="/wiki/Tower_Building_(New_York_City)" title="Tower Building (New York City)">Tower Building</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/Bradford_Gilbert" title="Bradford Gilbert">Bradford Gilbert</a> and built in 1889, is considered by some to be New York City's first skyscraper, and may have been the first building in New York City to use a skeletal steel frame,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> while the <a href="/wiki/Home_Insurance_Building" title="Home Insurance Building">Home Insurance Building</a> in Chicago, which was built in 1884, is considered the world's first skyscraper due to its steel skeleton).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> Subsequent buildings such as the <a href="/wiki/Singer_Building" title="Singer Building">Singer Building</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Life_Tower" title="Metropolitan Life Tower">Metropolitan Life Tower</a> were higher still.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Woolworth_Building" title="Woolworth Building">Woolworth Building</a>, a neo-Gothic "Cathedral of Commerce" overlooking City Hall, was designed by <a href="/wiki/Cass_Gilbert" title="Cass Gilbert">Cass Gilbert</a>. At 792 feet (241 m), it became the world's tallest building upon its completion in 1913, an honor it retained until 1930, when it was overtaken by <a href="/wiki/40_Wall_Street" title="40 Wall Street">40 Wall Street</a>.</li>
<li>That same year, the <a href="/wiki/Chrysler_Building" title="Chrysler Building">Chrysler Building</a> took the lead as the tallest building in the world, scraping the sky at 1,046 feet (319 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Designed by <a href="/wiki/William_Van_Alen" title="William Van Alen">William Van Alen</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> style masterpiece with an exterior crafted of brick,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> the Chrysler Building continues to be a favorite of New Yorkers to this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_Building" title="Empire State Building">Empire State Building</a>, the first building to have more than 100 floors (it has 102), was completed the following year. It was designed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shreve,_Lamb_and_Harmon" title="Shreve, Lamb and Harmon">Shreve, Lamb and Harmon</a> in the contemporary Art Deco style. The tower takes its name from the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_nicknames" title="List of U.S. state nicknames">nickname</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_State" title="New York State">New York State</a>. Upon its completion in 1931 at 1,250 feet (381 m), it took the top spot as tallest building, and towered above all other buildings until 1972. The antenna mast added in 1951 brought pinnacle height to 1,472 feet (449 m), lowered in 1984 to 1,454 feet (443 m).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pollak_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-pollak-68">[68]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/World_Trade_Center_(1973%E2%80%932001)" title="World Trade Center (1973–2001)">World Trade Center</a> officially reached full height in 1972, was completed in 1973, and consisted of two tall towers and several smaller buildings. For a short time, the first of the two towers was the world's tallest building. Upon completion, the towers stood for 28 years, until the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> destroyed the buildings in 2001. Various governmental entities, financial firms, and law firms called the towers home.</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Willis_Tower" title="Willis Tower">Willis Tower</a> (formerly Sears Tower) was completed in 1974, one year after the World Trade Center, and surpassed it as the world's tallest building. It was the first building to employ the "<a href="/wiki/Tube_(structure)" title="Tube (structure)">bundled tube</a>" structural system, designed by Fazlur Khan. The building was not surpassed in height until the <a href="/wiki/Petronas_Towers" title="Petronas Towers">Petronas Towers</a> were constructed in 1998, but remained the tallest in some categories until <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a> surpassed it in all categories in 2010. It is currently the second tallest building in the United States, after <a href="/wiki/One_World_Trade_Center" title="One World Trade Center">One World Trade Center</a>, which was built to replace the destroyed towers.</li>
</ul>
<p>Momentum in setting records passed from the United States to other nations with the opening of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petronas_Twin_Towers" title="Petronas Twin Towers">Petronas Twin Towers</a> in Kuala Lumpur, Malaysia, in 1998. The record for the world's tallest building has remained in Asia since the opening of <a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> in Taipei, Taiwan, in 2004. A number of architectural records, including those of the world's tallest building and tallest free-standing structure, moved to the Middle East with the opening of the <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a> in Dubai, United Arab Emirates.</p>
<p>This geographical transition is accompanied by a change in approach to skyscraper design. For much of the twentieth century large buildings took the form of simple geometrical shapes. This reflected the "international style" or <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modernist</a> philosophy shaped by <a href="/wiki/Bauhaus" title="Bauhaus">Bauhaus</a> architects early in the century. The last of these, the Willis Tower and World Trade Center towers in New York, erected in the 1970s, reflect the philosophy. Tastes shifted in the decade which followed, and new skyscrapers began to exhibit <a href="/wiki/Postmodernism" title="Postmodernism">postmodernist</a> influences. This approach to design avails itself of historical elements, often adapted and re-interpreted, in creating technologically modern structures. The Petronas Twin Towers recall Asian <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagoda</a> architecture and Islamic geometric principles. Taipei 101 likewise reflects the <a href="/wiki/Pagoda" title="Pagoda">pagoda</a> tradition as it incorporates ancient <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motifs</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Ruyi_(scepter)" title="Ruyi (scepter)">ruyi</a> symbol. The Burj Khalifa draws inspiration from traditional <a href="/wiki/Islamic_art" title="Islamic art">Islamic art</a>. Architects in recent years have sought to create structures that would not appear equally at home if set in any part of the world, but that reflect the culture thriving in the spot where they stand.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>The following list measures height of the roof.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2014)">not in citation given</span></a></i>]</sup> The more common gauge is the "highest architectural detail"; such ranking would have included Petronas Towers, built in 1998.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancellation">Cancellation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Cancellation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many skyscrapers were never built due to financial problems, politics and culture. The <a href="/wiki/Chicago_Spire" title="Chicago Spire">Chicago Spire</a> was to be the tallest building in the Western Hemisphere, but it was on hold due to the global financial crisis of 2008. One year later, the project was cancelled.</p>
<ul>
<li>The 610-metre (2,000 ft) <a href="/wiki/Russia_Tower" title="Russia Tower">Russia Tower</a> was cancelled due to the global financial crisis of 2008. It would have dominated the <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> skyline.</li>
<li>Proposed in 1989, The <a href="/wiki/Miglin-Beitler_Skyneedle" title="Miglin-Beitler Skyneedle">Miglin-Beitler Skyneedle</a> of Chicago would have been the tallest freestanding in the world. But it was never built due to the Persian gulf war.</li>
<li>The cancelled <a href="/wiki/Chicago_World_Trade_Center" title="Chicago World Trade Center">Chicago World Trade Center</a> would have been the first skyscraper to exceed a height of 700 metres (2,300 ft). It was proposed in the 1990s, a second one was to be completed but never built.</li>
<li>The construction of the 200-floor, Dubai's <a href="/wiki/Nakheel_Tower" title="Nakheel Tower">Nakheel Tower</a>—which was planned to be taller than 1,000 metres (3,300 ft)—was halted due to financial problems; 18 months later the project was cancelled.</li>
<li>Harry Grant's <a href="/wiki/Grant_USA_Tower" title="Grant USA Tower">Grant USA Tower</a> in Newark, New Jersey was never built due to bankruptcy, the tower would have more than 550 meters tall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></li>
<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Project_2000_Tower&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Project 2000 Tower (page does not exist)">Project 2000 Tower</a> in Chicago was cancelled and never built, it would have had a height of 610 metres (2,000 ft)</li>
<li>The proposed <a href="/wiki/1_New_York_Place" title="1 New York Place">1 New York Place</a> would have had a height of 320 metres (1,050 ft), but was never built.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vision_Brisbane" title="Vision Brisbane">Vision Brisbane</a> was to be the tallest building in <a href="/wiki/Brisbane" title="Brisbane">Brisbane</a> and third tallest in <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a> if built. It was cancelled due to the 2008 global financial crisis and the site was eventually sold where construction of a skyscraper of similar height was approved.</li>
<li>The original design of Hong Kong's <a href="/wiki/International_Commerce_Centre" title="International Commerce Centre">International Commerce Centre</a>, with a height of 574 metres (1,883 ft), was supposed to have a crystal facade. It was changed and decreased to 484 metres (1,588 ft) due to height restrictions.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Future_developments">Future developments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Future developments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Proposed_tall_buildings_and_structures" title="Proposed tall buildings and structures">Proposed tall buildings and structures</a></div>
<p>At the time Taipei 101 broke the half-km mark in height, it was already technically possible to build structures towering over a km above the ground.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Proposals for such structures have been put forward, including the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mubarak_al-Kabir_Tower" title="Mubarak al-Kabir Tower">Burj Mubarak Al Kabir</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan_Tower" title="Azerbaijan Tower">Azerbaijan Tower</a> in <a href="/wiki/Baku" title="Baku">Baku</a>. Kilometer-plus structures present architectural challenges that may eventually place them in a new architectural category.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sadek_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sadek-71">[71]</a></sup> The first building under construction and planned to be over one kilometre tall is the <a href="/wiki/Jeddah_Tower" title="Jeddah Tower">Jeddah Tower</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Wooden_skyscrapers">Wooden skyscrapers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Skyscraper&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Wooden skyscrapers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Several wooden skyscraper designs have been designed and built, with the tallest currently being built is a 14-story housing project in <a href="/wiki/Bergen" title="Bergen">Bergen, Norway</a> known as 'Treet' or 'The Tree' to be finished November 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Other buildings include Brock Commons an 18-story wooden <a href="/wiki/Dormitory" title="Dormitory">dormitory</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_British_Columbia" title="University of British Columbia">University of British Columbia</a> in <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> to be completed in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> A 40-story residential building 'Trätoppen' has been proposed by architect Anders Berensson to be built in <a href="/wiki/Stockholm" title="Stockholm">Stockholm, Sweden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>
<p>Wooden skyscrapers are estimated to be around a quarter of the weight of an equivalent <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_concrete" title="Reinforced concrete">reinforced-concrete</a> structure as well as reducing the building carbon footprint by 60–75%. Buildings have been designed using <a href="/wiki/Cross_laminated_timber" title="Cross laminated timber">cross-laminated timber</a> (CLT) which gives a higher rigidity and strength to wooden structures.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-72">[72]</a></sup> CLT panels are prefabricated and can therefore speed up building time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>

<ul>
<li><cite class="citation web">Adam, Robert. <a class="external text" href="http://www.city-journal.org/html/12_2_urbanities-how_to_build.html" rel="nofollow">"How to Build Skyscrapers"</a>. <i>City Journal</i><span class="reference-accessdate">.
